SECTION 1.  
PROJECT CONSIDERATIONS 
The proposed Homeland Defense Radar – Hawaii (HDR-H) project site is located at the 
Pacific Missile Range Facility (PMRF) Site 4 on the Island of Kauai, Hawaii. The project location 
and general vicinity are shown on the Project Location Map, Plate 1. 
Based on the information provided, it is desired to develop existing mostly vacant land 
within the PMRF site on the Island of Kauai, Hawaii. We understand the project entails 
construction and installation of several site facilities and a utilities distribution system. In 
addition, relocation of existing structures and antennas will be required. The following 
summarizes the improvements planned within the PMRF Site 4 for this project. 
1. 
Several structures including a Radar Equipment Shelter, Mission Control Facility, 
Thermal Control System, Modernization of Enterprise Terminal (MET), Entry 
Control Facility (ECF), Near Field Antenna, Civil Engineering and Security Forces 
Building, Maintenance Facility, Fire Station, and Barracks/Dormitory. 
2. 
New utility infrastructure including a Power Plant, Bulk Fuel Storage, Water 
Supply 
Building, 
Electrical 
Substation, 
ISFAC, 
In-Flight 
Interceptor 
Communications System Data Terminal (IDT) Switchgear Enclosure, IDT Support 
Facility, and a Fuel Unloading Facility. We understand most of the planned 
structures will be constructed on the top of a raised embankment of 
approximately 18 to 20 feet of fill materials. The sides of this raised fill 
embankment also will serve as a tsunami berm for the project site. 
3. 
Relocation of Buildings 802 and 803. 
4. 
Three new antennas to replace Antennas 822, 851, 852, 853, and 854. 
5. 
Antenna Towers 823 and 850. 
6. 
Buildings 644, 803, 846, a fabric-covered shelter, and golf driving range, 
Building 848, Building 849, Buildings 855 and 857, Building 944, Archery Range, 
Plant Nursery, and Launch Facility 641 (a 100,000-pound launch rail).  
7. 
Improvements to the existing Lighthouse Road. 
8. 
Construction of new HDR-H access roads.  
A design-level field exploration and laboratory testing program was undertaken to assist 
the design team in the design of the project. A summary of the field exploration program for

SECTION 2.  
SCOPE OF WORK 
The purpose of our field exploration and laboratory testing program is to obtain an 
overview of the surface and subsurface conditions at the Homeland Defense Radar – Hawaii 
(HDR-H) project at the Pacific Missile Range Facility (PMRF) project site to develop a 
geotechnical data report for use in the planning and design of the project. 
Our work was performed in general accordance with the scope of services outlined in 
our revised fee proposal dated August 14, 2020 and the subconsultant agreement entered into 
on December 24, 2020. In order to accomplish this, we conducted a field exploration and 
laboratory testing program consisting of the following tasks and efforts: 
1. 
Research and review of in-house geologic and soils information in the project 
vicinity. A list of the references used in the preparation of this geotechnical data 
report is presented in the reference section. 
2. 
Application for excavation permits from the applicable agencies and 
coordination of site access and underground utility toning. 
3. 
Procurement of base passes for our field personnel and subcontractors for 
access to the project site. 
4. 
Preparation and submittal of an Accident Prevention Plan with Activity Hazard 
Analysis in support of our field exploration activities and briefing of our field 
personnel of the plan. 
5. 
Site reconnaissance to review the boring locations, geologic conditions, and 
access considerations for the drilling exploration. 
6. 
Toning of underground utilities by our subcontractor, Haggith’s Structural Scan & 
Inspections, LLC., and initiation of Hawaii One Call process for utility clearance by 
our field engineers and field geologists. 
7. 
Provision of safety devices around the work area. 
8. 
Performance of energy measurement of the standard penetration test (SPT) 
automatic trip hammer system of the drill rig involved in this geotechnical 
exploration in accordance with ASTM D4633. 
9. 
Mobilization and demobilization of a truck-mounted drill rig and two operators 
from Honolulu to the project site and back. 
GDR for HDR-H PMRF Site 4 - 02/28/2022
SECTION 2. SCOPE OF WORK 
W.O. 8192-00 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 5 
Hawaii ⚫ California 
10. 
Mobilization and demobilization of a water truck and one operator to the project 
site and back. 
11. 
Installation of a grounding rod at each boring location to provide a grounding 
source for the truck-mounted drill rig. The purpose of the electrical grounding is 
a safety measure to mitigate the potential for radio induction from the nearby 
communication towers at the project site to the truck-mounted drill rig. 
12. 
Drilling and sampling of 74 borings extending to depths of about 6 to 101.5 feet 
below the existing ground surface for a total of about 4,565 linear feet of 
exploration. 
13. 
Performance of seismic shear wave velocity profiling using seismic cone 
penetration testing (SCPT) equipment at one selected boring (Boring No. B-6) 
located at the Homeland Defense Radar Equipment Shelter site to a depth of 
about 101.5 feet for seismic design considerations.  
14. 
Performance of 30 in-situ permeability tests at depths of approximately 15, 25, 
and 35 feet below the existing ground surface in Boring Nos. 1, 3, 5, 7, and 9 and 
at depths of approximately 10, 20, and 30 feet below the existing ground surface 
in Boring Nos. 2, 4, 6, 8, and 10. 
15. 
Percolation testing at the bottom of Boring Nos. DOH-1 through DOH-3 and 
DOH-201 (at approximately 6 feet below the existing ground surface) in 
accordance with the State of Hawaii, Department of Health’s Administrative 
Rules, Chapter 11-62-31.2 and Chapter 10 of the Ten States Standards. 
16. 
Installation of five vibrating wire piezometers for monitoring the groundwater 
levels on an hourly basis. Piezometers were installed in Boring Nos. B-122(P), 
B-133(P), B-137(P), B-139(P), and B-141(P). 
17. 
Backfilling of the drilled borings with cement grout upon completion of each 
boring, with the exception of the seismic shear wave velocity test location, which 
was backfilled with bentonite. 
18. 
Performance of a Global Navigation Satellite System (GNSS) survey of each 
boring location (rated to within about 1-foot horizontal accuracy) following 
boring completion. 
19. 
Performance of in-situ soil resistivity surveys at eighteen selected locations using 
the Wenner 4-Pin Method along orthogonal lines at 1, 3, 5, 10, 15 and 30-foot 
electrode spacings in general accordance with ASTM G57 to evaluate the 
average resistivity characteristics of the subsurface materials to assist in the 
design of a grounding system by others.

SECTION 3.  
SITE CHARACTERIZATION 
Of interest for the geotechnical analysis are the subsurface materials encountered at 
the project site, the engineering properties of the materials encountered, and the variability of 
the subsurface conditions across the project site. Therefore, the following subsections provide a 
description of the geologic setting of the project site, the surface and subsurface conditions 
encountered at the site, and a discussion of the items needed for seismic evaluation and 
design, such as the seismicity, soil liquefaction, and the soil profile characteristics for seismic 
analysis. 
3.1 
Regional Geology 
The Island of Kaua`i is a dissected basaltic dome that was initially formed by a single 
large shield volcano, which has subsequently been deeply eroded and partially covered with 
later (younger) volcanic materials. The Island of Kaua`i is the oldest of the main Hawaiian 
Islands and today encompasses approximately 562 square miles in area with a maximum 
elevation of about +5,148 feet Mean Sea Level (MSL) at Mt. Wai’ale’ale near the center of the 
island. It is believed that during the Tertiary Period (about 5 to 6 million years ago in the 
Pliocene Epoch), basaltic lavas belonging to the Waimea Canyon Volcanic Series built a roughly 
circular island volcano from the ocean floor that was estimated to have been about 20,000 feet 
deep at the time. Near the end of the Pliocene Epoch, this main shield building volcanic phase 
came to a halt. 
Following a long period of erosion and island mass subsidence, volcanism was renewed 
during the early Pleistocene Epoch (about 1.5 million years ago) with the eruption of the Koloa 
Volcanic Series from multiple vents throughout the island. This post-erosional volcanic activity 
laid a veneer of fresh lava and interbedded volcanic sediments over the older deposits of the 
Waimea Canyon Volcanic Series. Because of the long period of erosion between the eruption of 
the Waimea Canyon Volcanic Series and the Koloa Volcanic Series, deposits of the Koloa 
Volcanic Series typically filled the erosional valleys and depressions that existed at that time. 
Consequently, the basaltic rocks of the Koloa Volcanic Series are commonly found overlying 
thick residual soil and alluvial deposits. 
GDR for HDR-H PMRF Site 4 - 02/28/2022
SECTION 3. SITE CHARACTERIZATION  
W.O. 8192-00 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 8 
Hawaii ⚫ California 
The dominant geologic processes of weathering, landform erosion, and sediment 
re-deposition followed during the late Pleistocene and Recent Epochs. Combined with the 
erosional and depositional effects of the Pleistocene sea-level fluctuations, which occurred in 
response to the worldwide advance and retreat of the continental ice sheets, the Island of 
Kaua`i has evolved to its present form. 
The project site is on a low-lying coastal area of Mana Plain on the west side of the 
island. Mana Plain was below sea level during the Pleistocene Epoch and is characterized by 
both terrigenous alluvium and calcareous marine deposits. The calcareous deposits were 
formed in a shallow lagoonal environment behind a beach ridge. 
3.2 
Site Description 
The project site is situated on the south side of PMRF near the intersection of 
Nohili Road and Kolekole Point Road. In general, the majority of the project site is covered with 
light vegetation consisting of short grasses, with the exception of portions of the northern and 
eastern areas of the project site, which are heavily vegetated with Haole Koa and Kiawe trees.  
Landscaped shrubs and trees were observed in the vicinity of the existing Building 801 
and the MWR (Morale, Welfare, and Recreation) driving range. Existing antenna structures 
were observed throughout the southern portion of the project site. Based on the topographic 
survey provided, the project site is relatively flat with existing ground surface elevations 
generally ranging between about +10 and +13 feet MSL. 
3.3 
Subsurface Conditions 
We explored the subsurface conditions across the project site by drilling and sampling 
74 borings, designated as Boring Nos. B-1 through B-10, B-101 through B-142, B-201 through 
B-203, B-301 through B-309, B-501 through B-506, DOH-1 through DOH-3, and DOH-201 
extending to depths of about 6 to 101.5 feet below the existing pavement surface. Six bulk 
samples of the near-surface soils, designated as Bulk-101, Bulk-102, Bulk-203, Bulk-502, 
Bulk-504, and Bulk-506, were obtained to evaluate the pavement support characteristics and 
moisture-density relationships of the near-surface soils. The approximate boring and bulk 
GDR for HDR-H PMRF Site 4 - 02/28/2022
SECTION 3. SITE CHARACTERIZATION

Page 9 
Hawaii ⚫ California 
sample locations are shown on the Overall Boring Location Plan (Plate 2) and Boring Location 
Plan (Plates 3.1 through 3.9). 
Our field exploration generally encountered 0.25 to 4 feet of surface fill materials 
underlain by beach deposits. The surface fill materials generally consisted of very loose to 
dense sands, silty sands, and silty gravel, and soft to stiff clayey silts and sandy silts. The beach 
deposits generally consisted of loose to very dense sands with minor amounts of silt particles 
(<12% passing the No. 200 sieve) extending to depths of about 12 to 43 feet below the existing 
ground surface. A pavement section consisting of 1 to 2.5 inches of asphaltic concrete 
pavement overlying 1 to 8 inches of base material was encountered in Boring Nos. B-106, B-
108, and B-501 through B-505. 
The beach deposits generally were underlain by old lagoonal deposits consisting of 
medium dense to very dense sands, silty sands, and sandy gravel extending down to about 31.5 
to 51 feet below the ground surface. The old lagoonal deposits generally were underlain by 
interbedded zones of coralline detritus, coral formation, coralline sandstone, alluvium, and 
alluvium with coralline debris extending to the maximum depth explored of about 101.5 feet 
below the existing pavement surface. 
The coralline detritus generally consisted of loose to very dense sands, silty sands, sandy 
gravel, and silty gravel. The coralline sandstone and coral formation encountered generally 
ranged from extremely to slightly weathered and very soft to hard. The alluvial deposits 
generally consisted of very soft to hard sandy clays, silty clays, clayey silts, and sandy silts, and 
loose to very dense clayey sands, clayey gravel, silty sands, and silty gravel.  
Detailed descriptions of our field exploration methodology and the Logs of Borings are 
presented in Appendix A. Results of the laboratory tests performed on selected soil samples 
and rock core samples are presented in Appendix G. Photographs of the core samples retrieved 
from our field exploration are presented in Appendix H.

3.4 
Subsurface Characteristics 
In general, we observed two main stratigraphic units across the project area. The 
stratigraphic units consist of soil and rock materials of varying character and engineering 
properties. The two main stratigraphic units are listed below in the general order of geologic 
age, beginning with the younger unit and ending with the older unit: 
1. 
Coral Limestone (Qls) 
2. 
Older Alluvium (Qa) 
It should be noted that the surface fills generally are underlain by beach deposits and 
old lagoonal deposits, and interbedded layers of coralline detritus, coral/sandstone formations, 
alluvium, and alluvium with coralline debris. The following subsections briefly describe the 
general characteristics of each of the above stratigraphic units based on our literature research, 
experience with similar materials, and the field exploration and laboratory testing program 
conducted at selected locations across the project site. The approximate delineation of the 
stratigraphic subunits at the ground surface of the project vicinity is presented on the Geology 
Map, Plate 4. 
3.4.1 Coral Limestone (Qls) 
This unit consists of fossil remnants of coral-algal reefs and their related detrital debris 
deposits. In Hawaii, the detrital deposits tend to be the dominant portion of any 
coralline rock assemblage. The detrital deposits vary from weakly to strongly cemented 
coralline debris consisting of gravel, sand, and silt-sized clastic fragments. Some of the 
coralline fragments were dissolved, and the solution cavities were filled with cemented 
and/or uncemented fine sands and silts. The following subunits are used in this 
geotechnical data report. 
Beach Deposits (Qbd) 
This subunit consists primarily granular materials broken from coral reef 
formations and marine shells by wave action. The soils are primarily calcareous 
and generally consist of loose to dense, poorly graded sands and gravel. 
GDR for HDR-H PMRF Site 4 - 02/28/2022
SECTION 3. SITE CHARACTERIZATION  
W.O. 8192-00 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 11 
Hawaii ⚫ California 
Coralline Detritus 
This subunit consists of granular detritus broken from reef formations. The soils 
generally consist of medium dense to dense, poorly graded sands and gravel. 
Silts and clays are present only in minor or trace amounts. Detritus also may be 
weakly cemented with occasional moderate to strong cementation. 
Coral Formation 
This subunit is composed of coral and calcareous algae in their original growth 
positions or detrital deposits, which have become moderately to well cemented. 
Sandstone 
Sandstone is a sedimentary rock composed of mainly cemented sand-sized 
particles (clasts) consisting of individual mineral particles or grains of the 
weathered parent rock. The sedimentary particles comprising sandstone 
typically are derived from the breakdown of parent material into sedimentary 
particles. The sedimentary particles typically are eroded, transported, and sorted 
by water or air, and deposited as layers of sedimentary materials. Over time, the 
sediments may be buried by other sediments or geologic layers, and the burial 
pressure combined with a cementing agent act to consolidate the grains and 
cement the pore space between the grains to form hardened sandstone. Typical 
cementing agents may be calcium carbonate (common in calcareous sand 
deposits of Hawaii) or silica and some clays.  
Most sandstones in Hawaii are light brown in color and calcareous being derived 
from the breakdown of parent coralline and reef materials into sand-sized 
particles. The cementing agent in most calcareous sandstone is calcium 
carbonate. Inclusions of basaltic rock fragments, marine shells, and other 
fragments of broken coral may be cemented in the sandstone layers. Sandstones 
containing such clast inclusions commonly are referred to as “beachrock” in 
Hawaii, having been derived from available shoreline geologic materials.  
GDR for HDR-H PMRF Site 4 - 02/28/2022
SECTION 3. SITE CHARACTERIZATION  
W.O. 8192-00 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 12 
Hawaii ⚫ California 
The strength (hardness) of sandstone generally depends on the grain size, 
degree of sorting, and degree of cementation of the sedimentary materials. The 
weathering and decomposition of calcareous sandstone may be accelerated by 
exposure to acidic water, which attacks the calcareous cement binding the sand 
grains and causes a slow exfoliation of the outermost exposed layers of the 
sandstone outcropping. Sandstone may or may not contain visible bedding 
surfaces. Aeolian (wind derived) sandstone may exhibit cross-bedding structures 
that developed during the initial deposition of the sand layers. 
3.4.2 Older Alluvium (Qa) 
Older alluvial deposits are generally very stiff to hard silty and clayey soils that originally 
were deposited in alluvial environments, buried over long periods of time by more 
recent deposits to become consolidated, and then subsequently uncovered at the 
ground surface by erosion or other geologic processes. This unit generally overlies the 
Napali Formation of the Waimea Canyon Volcanic Series and consists of terrigenous 
sediments, which have been transported by stream action from the upper reaches of 
the streams.  
Gravel lenses representing buried stream channels are sometimes encountered. 
Portions of the Older Alluvium unit contain some weathered basaltic cobbles and 
boulders. These coarse-grained parts of the unit also may contain artesian groundwater 
from the basal freshwater system or as a sub-aquifer. The deposits may resemble 
saprolitic soil deposits, but they are clearly differentiable by the layered characteristics. 
Frequently, these materials show a moderate to high shrink/swell potential when 
subjected to fluctuations in moisture. The following subunits are used in this 
geotechnical data report. 
Older Alluvium 
This subunit generally consists of tan to brown fat clays with varying amounts of 
sands and infrequent pockets of gravel and cobbles. The consistency is generally 
in the range of very stiff to hard.  
GDR for HDR-H PMRF Site 4 - 02/28/2022
SECTION 3. SITE CHARACTERIZATION  
W.O. 8192-00 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 13 
Hawaii ⚫ California 
Old Lagoonal Deposit 
This subunit generally consists of light gray to medium gray sandy silts or silty 
sands in stiff to very stiff consistency and/or medium dense to dense relative 
density with various degrees of cementation. In some cases, strongly cemented 
deposits form siltstones. Gravel-sized fragments are rare. Frequently, the silts 
are organic in origin and may have a mild to strong organic odor.  
3.4.3 Groundwater 
Groundwater level measurements were taken during the advancement of the borings 
drilled for the project during our field exploration program. We encountered 
groundwater level in the drilled borings at depths ranging from about 6.6 to 11.3 feet 
below the existing ground surface. The groundwater levels measured generally 
correspond to about Elevations +1.4 to +4.9 feet MSL. It should be noted that 
groundwater levels are subject to change due to tidal fluctuations, storm surge 
conditions, rainfall, time of year, seasonal precipitation, surface water runoff, and other 
factors. 
END OF SITE CHARACTERIZATION

SECTION 4.  
GEOTECHNICAL EXPLORATION PROGRAM 
The geotechnical exploration program conducted for the project generally consisted of 
the following field and laboratory work components. A brief description of each work 
component is presented below. Detailed discussions, findings, and test results of the various 
components are provided in the respective appendices herein. 
4.1 
Soil Borings, Rock Coring, and Associated Field Testing 
The field exploration is described in more detail in the introduction to Appendix A. The 
following discussion provides an overview of the methods and procedures. Typically, soil 
borings were advanced with 4-inch diameter continuous-flight solid-stem augers (SSA) to the 
first encounter with groundwater and then continued with PQ-size wireline coring methods to 
advance the PQ-size casing from the ground surface. In general, auger drilling was used in areas 
where medium dense to very dense granular soils exist and in locations where rock coring was 
not anticipated. Additionally, SSA drilling was switched over to the wireline coring method 
when coralline detritus or coral formations were encountered or when soft and/or loose soils 
that caved in were encountered. 
Sampling of the soils was performed using AWJ drill rods (1.75 OD/1.25 ID) at about 
5 to 10-foot intervals. Drive samples were obtained using the modified California sampler 
(ASTM D3550) and the Standard Penetration Test (SPT) split-spoon sampler (ASTM D1586).  
Where the boring was advanced utilizing the PQ core barrel in the soil formations, 
samples of the soils recovered from the PQ core barrel also were retained in core boxes for 
visual observation. For intervals where the boring was advanced utilizing the PQ casing only as a 
rotary wash drilling method, there were no soil samples collected. The borings were advanced 
into the rock formations with the PQ-size wireline coring methods. The PQ-size wireline coring 
methods produce rock core samples of 3.35 inches (85 mm) in diameter. 
In-situ testing of the subsurface materials in the borings included the SPT, which was 
performed in accordance with ASTM D1586. Standard Penetration Test (SPT) sampling provides 
a measure of the resistance or consistency of a soil and is reported as the uncorrected N-value, 
GDR for HDR-H PMRF Site 4 - 02/28/2022
SECTION 4. GEOTECHNICAL EXPLORATION PROGRAM  
W.O. 8192-00 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 15 
Hawaii ⚫ California 
which is defined as the number of blows required to drive a standard split-spoon sampler 
12 inches with a 140-lb. hammer, after 6 inches of initial seating. The penetration resistance of 
the Modified California Sampler was recorded in a similar manner, and the Modified California 
Sampler blows per 12 inches can be approximately related to the SPT N-value through 
correlation. Blow count correlation between the Modified California Sampler and the Standard 
Penetration Test Sampler is presented in Appendix J. 
Rock core recovery (REC) and rock quality designation (RQD) were measured as 
described in Appendix A. ASTM D6032 - Standard Test Method for Determining Rock Quality 
Designation (RQD) of Rock Core states that “Core sizes from BQ to PQ with core diameters of 
36.5 mm (1.44 in.) to 85 mm (3.35 in.), respectively, are normally acceptable for measuring RQD 
as long as proper drilling techniques are used that do not cause excessive core breakage or 
poor recovery, or both. The NX-size (54.7 mm [2.16 in.]) and NQ-size (47.5 mm [1.87 in.]) are 
the optimal core sizes for measuring RQD. The RQD is also useful for large core diameters 
provided the core diameter is clearly stated.” The rock core photographs are included in 
Appendix H for information and reference. 
4.2 
Seismic Shear Wave Velocity Testing 
Seismic shear wave velocity profiling was performed within one completed boring 
(Boring No. 6) using seismic cone penetration testing (SCPT) equipment. Procedures for the 
tests are discussed in more detail in Appendix B. In brief, the completed boring was tremie-
filled with time-release bentonite pellets and bentonite chips in order to provide a medium 
through which the seismic cone tip could be pushed. The sleeve and tip resistance through the 
bentonite-filled boring is not meaningful because the cone is pushed through the bentonite 
backfill; however, the seismic wave travels from the ground surface through the surrounding 
soils, allowing measurement of the average seismic shear wave velocity. 
The seismic cone tip contains a geophone sensor. At a succession of depths, the 
advancement of the cone tip was halted, a seismic pulse was generated at the ground surface 
by striking a loaded plank with a switched hammer, and the resulting seismic shear wave was 
detected at the geophone. The distance from the source and the wave arrival time allow for 
GDR for HDR-H PMRF Site 4 - 02/28/2022
SECTION 4. GEOTECHNICAL EXPLORATION PROGRAM  
W.O. 8192-00 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 16 
Hawaii ⚫ California 
determination of the average shear wave velocity over a depth interval. The measured average 
seismic shear wave velocity logs are presented in Appendix B. 
4.3 
Piezometric Water Level Monitoring 
Depths to groundwater, where encountered, were measured and recorded during the 
exploration activities and summarized in Tables C-1.1 thru C-1.6. In addition, vibrating wire 
piezometers (Model 4500 manufactured by Geokon) were installed in five selected boring 
locations (Boring Nos. 122, 133, 137, 139 and 141) to monitor piezometric water levels and 
their seasonal fluctuations.  
Each boring was backfilled with cement-bentonite grout with an equipment mounting 
pole over the monitoring point. Data are recorded on a near-continuous basis using a Strand 
VW4 datalogger manufactured by Sensemetrics and transmitted via a wireless system for 
processing from the time of installation (May 2021) through a 9-month monitoring period. After 
termination of the monitoring period, the monitoring well will be left functional unless 
otherwise directed by USACE. Details of the piezometer installation and piezometric level 
readings are included in Appendix C. 
4.4 
Field Permeability Testing 
Field permeability tests (both constant head and falling head methods) were performed 
at depths of approximately 15, 25, and 35 feet below the existing ground surface in Boring Nos. 
1, 3, 5, 7, and 9, and at depths of approximately 10, 20, and 30 feet below the existing ground 
surface in Boring Nos. 2, 4, 6, 8, and 10. Details of the permeability test procedures and the 
calculated hydraulic conductivities are provided in Appendix D. 
4.5 
Field Percolation Testing 
Field percolation tests were performed in Borings DOH-1 through DOH-3 and DOH-201 
in general accordance with the procedures in Appendix C of the State of Hawaii, Department of 
Health’s Administrative Rules, Chapter 11-62-31.2, “Wastewater Systems” to evaluate the 
percolation characteristics of the subsurface soils for wastewater disposal. The approximate 
field percolation test locations are shown on the Overall Boring Location Plan (Plate 2) and 
GDR for HDR-H PMRF Site 4 - 02/28/2022
SECTION 4. GEOTECHNICAL EXPLORATION PROGRAM  
W.O. 8192-00 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 17 
Hawaii ⚫ California 
Boring Location Plan (Plates 3.1 through 3.9). The individual percolation test results are 
presented on the Site Evaluation/Percolation Test forms in Appendix E. 
The percolation tests were performed in the subsoil material by drilling a 4-inch 
diameter boring to a depth of about 6.5 feet below the existing ground surface. During testing, 
the hole was carefully filled with clean water to a minimum depth of 12 inches above the 
bottom of the hole. The time intervals and water level drops were recorded to provide field 
data upon which calculation of the percolation test was based. 
4.6 
Field Resistivity Survey 
A total of 216 field resistivity tests were conducted at a maximum electrode spacing of 
about 30 feet at 18 locations, designated as R-1 through R-15 and R-101 through R-103, using 
the in-situ Wenner 4-Pin Method and AEMC Models 4630 and 4672 resistance meters 
manufactured by AEMC Instruments. The approximate locations of the field resistivity tests are 
presented on the Overall Boring Location Plan (Plate 2) and Boring Location Plan (Plates 3.1 
through 3.9). 
The method utilizes four surface probes or pins (electrodes) to evaluate the average 
resistivity (an indication of corrosivity) of the underlying soil mass. The field resistivity surveys 
were used to evaluate the average resistivity values of the soil mass at the approximate survey 
locations. Results of the field resistivity surveys are presented in Appendix F.  
It should be noted that the field resistivity survey may be influenced by temperature, 
weather conditions, soil moisture, existing underground utilities, and other factors. Based on 
the results of our field resistivity surveys, the subsurface soils at the project site generally 
exhibited resistivity values ranging from about 1,149 to 461,708 ohm-cm. 
4.7 
Geotechnical Laboratory Testing 
Geotechnical laboratory testing was performed on soil and rock core samples to 
determine the index properties and the physical and engineering properties. Laboratory tests 
performed included moisture content, dry density, Atterberg Limits, grain size distribution, 
specific gravity, Direct Shear, Unconsolidated-Undrained (UU) Triaxial Compression, 
GDR for HDR-H PMRF Site 4 - 02/28/2022
SECTION 4. GEOTECHNICAL EXPLORATION PROGRAM  
W.O. 8192-00 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 18 
Hawaii ⚫ California 
Consolidated-Undrained (CU) Triaxial Compression, uniaxial compressive strength of rock, 
Consolidation, Soil Resistivity, Chemical Analyses (pH, chloride content, and sulfate content), 
Carbonate tests, Modified Proctor, and California Bearing Ratio. The laboratory test results are 
provided in Appendix G. 
A summary of the selected field tests and index property test results is presented in 
Table G-1 of Appendix G for ease of reference.

SECTION 5.  
LIMITATIONS 
The geotechnical field and laboratory test data submitted in this geotechnical data 
report are based upon information obtained from the field exploration points. The coordinates 
of the as-drilled boring locations indicated herein were obtained by using a hand-held Global 
Navigation Satellite System (GNSS) unit. Elevations of the field borings were obtained by 
interpolating between the spot elevations shown on the Topographic Survey Map transmitted 
by Sam O. Hirota, Inc. on October 1, 2021. 
The stratification lines shown on the graphic representations of the field exploration 
points depict the approximate boundaries between soil and rock types and, as such, may 
denote a gradual transition. Water level data presented on the logs of borings in this report 
were based on measurements recorded in the borings at the times shown on the graphic 
representations. It should be noted that fluctuation of the water level may occur due to 
variation in tides, rainfall, temperature, and other factors. 
This geotechnical data report has been prepared for the exclusive use of 
Sam O. Hirota, Inc. and their client, U.S. Army Corps of Engineers Honolulu District, for specific 
application to the Homeland Defense Radar- Hawaii (HDR-H), Pacific Missile Range Facility 
(PMRF) Site 4 project in accordance with generally accepted geotechnical engineering principles 
and practices. No warranty is expressed or implied.  
This geotechnical data report was not intended to investigate the potential for presence 
of hazardous materials existing at the project site. It should be noted that the equipment, 
techniques, and personnel used to conduct a geo-environmental exploration differ substantially 
from those applied in geotechnical engineering. 
END OF LIMITATIONS

APPENDIX A  
GDR for HDR-H PMRF Site 4 - 02/28/2022
W.O. 8192-00 
GEOLABS, INC. 
FEBRUARY 2022     Page A-1 
Hawaii • California 
A P P E N D I X   A 
Test Boring Exploration 
We explored the subsurface conditions at selected locations across the project site by 
drilling and sampling 74 borings, designated as Boring Nos. B-1 through B-10, B-101 through 
B-142, B-201 through B-203, B-301 through B-309, B-501 through B-506, DOH-1 through 
DOH-3, and DOH-201. The borings extended to depths of about 6 to 101.5 feet below the 
existing ground surface using truck-mounted drill rig equipped with continuous-flight augers 
and rotary coring tools. The field exploration was conducted from April 5 through September 
20, 2021. In addition, six bulk samples of the near-surface soils, designated as Bulk-101, 
Bulk-102, Bulk-203, Bulk-502, Bulk-504, and Bulk-506, were obtained for laboratory testing. The 
approximate boring and bulk sample locations are shown on the Overall Boring Location Plan 
(Plate 2) and Boring Location Plan (Plates 3.1 through 3.9). 
Soil borings were advanced with 4-inch diameter continuous-flight solid-stem augers 
(SSA) to the first encounter with groundwater and then continued with PQ-size wireline coring 
methods to advance the PQ-size casing from the ground surface. In general, auger drilling was 
used in areas where medium dense to very dense granular soils exist and in locations where 
rock coring was not anticipated. Additionally, SSA drilling was switched over to the wireline 
coring method when coralline detritus or coral formations were encountered or when soft 
and/or loose soils that caved in were encountered. After the borings were advanced to the 
maximum depth of the borings, the bored holes generally were backfilled with cement-grout 
with the exception of Boring No. B-6. 
Boring No. B-6, selected for seismic shear wave velocity testing, was backfilled with 
0.25-inch diameter time-release bentonite pellets (below the groundwater level) and bentonite 
chips (above the groundwater level). The temporary casing from the coring operations was 
used as a tremie pipe to place the time-release bentonite pellets starting from the bottom and 
advancing upward. Backfilling above the static groundwater table were performed in stages by 
adding water to limited amounts of bentonite chips to ensure hydration before additional 
bentonite chips were placed in the boring.  
Our field personnel classified the materials encountered in the borings by visual and 
textural examination in the field in general accordance with ASTM D2488, Standard Practice for 
Description and Identification of Soils, and monitored the drilling operations on a 
near-continuous basis. These classifications were further reviewed visually and by testing in the 
laboratory. Soils were classified in general accordance with ASTM D2487, Standard Practice for 
Classification of Soils for Engineering Purposes (Unified Soil Classification System), as shown on 
the Soil Log Legend, Plate A-0.1. Deviations made to the soil classification in accordance with 
ASTM D2487 are described on the Soil Classification Log Key, Plate A-0.2. Graphic 
representations of the materials encountered and other observations, such as groundwater 
levels, circulation water condition, drilling rate, etc. are presented on the Logs of Borings, 
Plates A-1 through A-74. 
GDR for HDR-H PMRF Site 4 - 02/28/2022
Appendix A 
Test Boring Exploration 
W.O. 8192-00 
GEOLABS, INC. 
FEBRUARY 2022     Page A-2 
Hawaii • California 
Relatively “undisturbed” soil samples were obtained from the borings drilled in general 
accordance with ASTM D3550, Standard Practice for Thick Wall, Ring-Lined, Spilt Barrel, Drive 
Sampling of Soils, by driving a 3-inch OD modified California sampler with a 140-pound hammer 
falling 30 inches. In addition, some samples were obtained from the borings drilled in general 
accordance with ASTM D1586, Standard Test Method for Penetration Test and Split-Barrel 
Sampling of Soils, by driving a 2-inch OD standard penetration sampler using the same hammer 
and drop. The blow counts needed to drive the sampler the second and third 6 inches of an 
18-inch drive are shown as the “Penetration Resistance” on the Logs of Borings at the 
appropriate sample depths. 
Core samples of the rock materials encountered at the project site were obtained by 
using diamond core drilling techniques, consisting of a PQ wireline coring system with double 
tube core barrel (core size of 3.35 inches), in general accordance with ASTM D2113, Diamond 
Core Drilling for Site Investigation. Core drilling is a rotary drilling method that uses a hollow bit 
to cut into the rock formation. The rock material left in the hollow core of the bit is 
mechanically recovered for examination and description. The coring rates indicated on the Logs 
of Borings refer to the time required to complete that particular core run at that depth. Rock 
cores were described in general accordance with the Rock Description System, as shown on the 
Rock Log Legend, Plate A-0.3. The Rock Description System is based generally on the publication 
“Suggested Methods for the Quantitative Description of Discontinuities in Rock Masses” by the 
International Society for Rock Mechanics (March 1977). 
Recovery (REC) is defined as the actual length of material recovered from a coring 
attempt versus the length of the core attempt. For example, if 3.7 feet of material is recovered 
from a 5.0-foot core run, the recovery would be 74 percent and would be shown on the Logs of 
Borings as REC = 74%. 
The Rock Quality Designation (RQD) is also a subjective guide to the relative quality of 
rock masses. RQD is defined as the percentage of the total core run in rock that is sound 
material in excess of 4 inches in length without any discontinuities, discounting any drilling, 
mechanical, and handling induced fractures or breaks. If 2.5 feet of sound material is recovered 
from a 5.0-foot core run in rock, the RQD would be 50 percent and would be shown on the Logs 
of Borings as RQD = 50%. It should be noted that RQD does not apply to coral material.  
However, the RQD values of the coral material are included in the boring logs for rough 
characterization of the coral material. Generally, the following is used to describe the relative 
quality of the rock based on the "Practical Handbook of Physical Properties of Rocks and 
Minerals” by Robert S. Carmichael (1989). 
Rock Quality 
RQD 
(%) 
Very Poor 
0 – 25 
Poor 
25 – 50

The rippability (or excavation characteristics) of a rock mass is a function of the relative 
hardness of the rock, its relative quality, brittleness, and fissile characteristics. Dense coral 
formations with high RQD values would be very difficult to rip and would probably require more 
arduous methods of excavation

COHESIVE SOIL (- #200    50%)
PRIMARY constituents are composed of the largest
percent of the soil mass. Primary constituents are
capitalized and bold (i.e., GRAVEL, SAND)
PRIMARY constituents are based on plasticity. Primary
constituents are capitalized and bold (i.e., CLAY, SILT)
SECONDARY constituents are composed of a
percentage less than the primary constituent. If the soil
mass consists of 12 percent or more fines content, a
cohesive constituent is used (SILTY or CLAYEY);
otherwise, a granular constituent is used (GRAVELLY
or SANDY) provided that the secondary constituent
consists of 20 percent or more of the soil mass.
Secondary constituents are capitalized and bold (i.e.,
SANDY GRAVEL, CLAYEY SAND) and precede the
primary constituent.
SECONDARY constituents are composed of a
percentage less than the primary constituent, but more
than 20 percent of the soil mass. Secondary constituents
are capitalized and bold (i.e., SANDY CLAY, SILTY
CLAY, CLAYEY SILT) and precede the primary
constituent.

Rock Log Legend
The following terms describe the resistance of a rock to indentation or scratching:
Specimen breaks with difficulty after several "pinging" hammer blows.
Example: Dense, fine grain volcanic rock
Specimen breaks with some difficulty after several hammer blows.
Example: Vesicular, vugular, coarse-grained rock
Specimen can be broked by one hammer blow. Cannot be scraped by knife. SPT may penetrate by
~25 blows per inch with bounce.
Example: Porous rock such as clinker, cinder, and coral reef
Can be indented by one hammer blow. Can be scraped or peeled by knife. SPT can penetrate by
~100 blows per foot.
Example: Weathered rock, chalk-like coral reef
Crumbles under hammer blow. Can be peeled and carved by knife. Can be indented by finger
pressure.
Example: Saprolite

Hawaii • California 
A P P E N D I X   B 
Seismic Shear Wave Velocity Tests 
Seismic shear wave velocity profiling of the subsurface materials was performed using 
Seismic Cone Penetration Testing (SCPT) equipment. Seismic shear wave velocity testing using 
seismic cone penetration testing equipment was performed at one boring locations, designated 
as Boring No. B-6 (SW), as shown on the Overall Boring Location Plan (Plate 2) and Boring 
Location Plan (Plates 3.1 through 3.9). 
The seismic shear wave velocity profiling was performed at approximately 1.4 to 
3.3-foot intervals extending to a depth of about 101.1 feet below the existing ground surface. In 
order to conduct the seismic shear wave velocity test in the boring, the test boring generally 
was advanced utilizing rotary coring methods to the maximum depth of the boring. Logs of the 
materials encountered in the boring are presented on the Logs of Boring in Appendix A. After 
the boring was advanced to the maximum depth of the boring, the bored hole was backfilled 
with 0.25-inch diameter time-release bentonite pellets. The temporary casing from the coring 
operations was used as a tremie pipe to place the bentonite pellets starting from the bottom 
and advancing upward. When the bentonite pellets are in contact with the groundwater in the 
boring, the pellets started to hydrate slowly. As the bentonite pellets hydrate, they swell and 
soften. Backfilling above the static groundwater table was performed in stages by adding water 
to a limited amount of bentonite chips to ensure hydration before additional bentonite chips 
were placed in the boring. The probe was then pushed through the softened bentonite 
extending to a depth of about 101.1 feet below the existing ground surface using the seismic 
cone penetration testing equipment (SCPT). 
The seismic shear wave velocity test consists of hydraulically pushing a 10-ton steel 
electronic subtraction cone into the bentonite backfilled boring. The cone has an apex angle of 
60 degrees and a projected surface area of 1.55 square inches (10 square centimeters) 
equipped with an electronic depth sensor attached to the cone rod. The seismic cone carries a 
uniaxial horizontal geophone to detect the arrival of a shear wave generated and propagated 
from the ground surface. Seismic measurements are made when the SCPT has stopped and a 
shear wave is sent into the subsurface. A shear wave is generated at the surface by striking a 
loaded plank with a switched hammer. The propagation time of the wave from the hammer 
blow to the cone was measured at each discrete depth interval. The vector difference of these 
depths divided by the time difference for the shear wave to arrive at the various depths 
provided the average shear wave velocity over the depth interval. 
The seismic shear wave velocities measured for the full depth, and the average seismic 
shear wave velocity calculated for the top 100 feet of the soil profile at the boring location are 
presented on Plates B-1.1 through B-1.5. The weighted average seismic shear wave velocity for 
the upper 100 feet of the soil profile was calculated based on the average shear wave velocity 
method described in Chapter 20 of the American Society of Civil Engineers (ASCE) 7-16, 
Minimum Design Loads for Buildings and Other Structures.

A P P E N D I X   C 
Groundwater Level Monitoring 
Groundwater levels were measured during advancement of the borings drilled for the 
project at the times shown on the Logs of Borings presented in Appendix A and summarized in 
Tables C-1.1 through C-1.6. 
In addition, groundwater monitoring points were established at five boring locations, 
including Boring Nos. B-122(P), B-133(P), B-137(P), B-139(P) and B-141(P), to monitor seasonal 
fluctuations. The boring locations where these groundwater monitoring points are established 
are shown on the Overall Boring Location Plan (Plate 2) and Boring Location Plan (Plates 3.1 
through 3.9). 
Five vibrating wire piezometers, Model 4500 manufactured by Geokon, Inc., were 
installed at selected depths within the five borings to monitor the groundwater levels on a 
near-continuous basis using a Strand datalogger manufactured by Sensemetrics. Catalog sheets 
of the vibrating wire piezometer and datalogger are presented in Figures C-1 and C-2 for 
illustration purposes only. The actual elevations of the vibrating wire piezometers are reflected 
on the installation details prepared for each monitoring point as shown on Plates C-1.1, C-2.1, 
C-3.1, C-4.1 and C-5.1. 
Prior to the installation, the filter stone is saturated by placing the piezometer under 
water a minimum of 12 hours and transported to the field in a submerged condition. In 
addition, zero readings were obtained in the field prior to the installation of each piezometer, 
which provided a baseline reference under field conditions and served as an acceptance test, 
which is a verification that the piezometer is working properly. The piezometers were attached 
to the outside of a 1-inch diameter PVC pipe at the desired depths and the assembly was then 
lowered into the boring. The entire boring with the assembly was then backfilled with a 
cement-bentonite grout up to the existing ground surface. The datalogger was mounted to a 
pole extending 5 to 6 feet above the existing ground surface. 
The groundwater level data are transmitted via a wireless system to cloud data storage 
on a near-continuous basis and processed in our office. Graphic presentations of the 
groundwater level data collected are provided on Plates C-1.2 through C-1.7, C-2.2 through 
C-2.7, C-3.2 through C-3.7, C-4.2 through C-4.7, and C-5.2 through C-5.7.

FIGURE C-1 
VIBRATING WIRE PIEZOMETERS 
Standard Piezometers 
Model 4500S(H), 4500AL(V) 
The Model 4500 Standard Piezometer is designed to measure fluid 
pressures such as ground water elevations and pore pressures when 
buried directly in embankments, fills, etc. Also for installation inside 
boreholes, observation wells and standard (>19 mm diameter) 
piezometer riser pipe. The Model 4500SH is designed with a heavy 
duty housing for pressures that exceed 3 MPa. The Model 4500AL is 
designed for low-pressure ranges. The vented version (ALV) provides 
automatic 
compensation 
for 
barometric 
pressure 
changes. 
Thermistors are included to measure temperatures.* 
Specifications 
4500S 
4500SH 
4500AL(V) 
es

A P P E N D I X   D 
Field Permeability Tests 
The permeability of the in-situ soils were evaluated by performing 30 constant head and 
thirty falling head tests in selected borings (Boring Nos. 1 through 10) at depths ranging from 
approximately 10 to 35 feet below the existing ground surface. 
The constant head tests were performed by pumping water into the borings until the 
water surface has stabilized and remained at equilibrium for a sufficient period of time. The 
water height and the rate of pumping were then measured to provide data upon which to base 
calculation of the subsoil permeability. The test results of the constant head permeability tests 
are presented on Plates D-1 through D-30. 
After completion of the constant head test, the water inside the boring was allowed to 
drop for the performance of a falling head test. The time intervals and water drops were 
recorded to provide data upon which to base calculation of the permeability values of the 
subsoils. The test results of the falling head permeability tests are presented on Plates D-31 
through D-60.

A P P E N D I X   E 
Field Percolation Tests 
Four field percolation tests were performed in Boring Nos. DOH-1 through DOH-3 and 
DOH-201 in general accordance with the procedures in Appendix C of the State of Hawaii, 
Department of Health’s Administrative Rules, Chapter 11-62-31.2, “Wastewater Systems” to 
evaluate the percolation characteristics of the subsurface soils for wastewater disposal. The 
approximate field percolation test locations are shown on the Overall Boring Location Plan 
(Plate 2) and Boring Location Plan (Plates 3.1 through 3.9). 
The percolation tests were performed in the subsoil material by drilling a 4-inch 
diameter boring to the specified depth of about 6 feet below the existing ground surface. Prior 
to testing, the percolation test holes were prepared by removing the smeared and loose 
materials from the sidewalls and bottom of the boring, respectively, and by placing about a 
1-inch-thick layer of coarse sand at the bottom of the boring.  
During testing, the hole was carefully filled with clean water to a minimum depth of 
6  inches above the bottom of the hole. The time intervals and water level drops were 
recorded to provide field data upon which calculation of the percolation rate was based. The 
percolation test results are presented on the Site Evaluation/Percolation Test forms, Plates E-1 
through E-4.

A P P E N D I X   F 
Field Resistivity Tests 
Geophysical survey equipment (i.e., resistance meter) was mobilized to the project site 
on April 15 and 16, 2020 and November 5, 2020 for the field resistivity survey. Two-hundred 
sixteen (216) field resistivity surveys were conducted at 18 locations, designated as R-1 through 
R-15 and R-101 through R-103, using the in-situ Wenner 4-Pin Method and AEMC Models 4630 
and 4672 resistance meters manufactured by AEMC Instruments. The method utilizes four 
surface probes or pins (electrodes) to evaluate the average resistivity of the underlying soil 
mass. The average resistivity values of the soil mass, extending from the existing ground surface 
to the test depth, was assessed by using this method at the approximate survey locations 
shown on the Overall Boring Location Plan, Plate 2. 
The soil resistivity surveys were performed along the existing ground surface at the 
approximate survey locations shown on the site plan. The average soil resistance along the 
proposed alignment was evaluated at each survey location with the electrodes aligned at equal 
spacing of generally 1, 3, 5, 10, 15, and 30 feet. The depth of penetration of a resistivity 
sounding is a function of the electrode spacing. 
The resistance meter (AEMC Models 4630 and 4672) provides a voltage between the 
outer electrodes, causing an electrical current to flow between them. The voltage drop 
between the inner electrodes is then measured by a sensitive, built-in voltmeter. As a result, 
the soil resistance (in ohms) is obtained from the voltmeter reading. The soil resistivity (in ohm-
cm), which is directly related to both resistance and spacing, can then be computed.  
The evaluated soil resistances and computed resistivity values are presented on 
Plates F-1 through F-15 and F-17 through F-19. In addition, the computed resistivity values with 
pin spacing at the test locations are presented in the graph on Plates F-16 and F-20. At the time 
of the field resistivity survey, the temperature was about 78º to 86º F. The existing ground 
surface was dry to damp. It should be noted that the soil resistivity may be influenced by 
temperature, weather conditions, existing underground utilities and other factors 
GDR for HDR-H PMRF Site 4 - 02/28/2022

APPENDIX G  
GDR for HDR-H PMRF Site 4 - 02/28/2022
W.O. 8192-00 
GEOLABS, INC.

Hawaii ⚫ California 
A P P E N D I X   G 
Geotechnical Laboratory Tests 
Samples recovered from our field exploration program performed for the project were 
visually examined and classified in our laboratory in Honolulu, Hawaii. Selected samples were 
tested to evaluate the physical characteristics and engineering properties of the soils 
encountered. 
The laboratory tests performed included determination of natural moisture content, dry 
density, Atterberg Limits, grain size distribution, specific gravity, Direct Shear, Unconsolidated-
Undrained (UU) Triaxial Compression, Consolidated-Undrained (CU) Triaxial Compression, 
uniaxial compressive strength of rock, Consolidation, Soil Resistivity, Chemical Analyses (pH, 
chloride content, and sulfate content), Carbonate tests, Modified Proctor, and California 
Bearing Ratio. Results of the tests performed are summarized in Tables G-1.1 through G-3 and 
are presented on Plates G-1 through G-141. Detailed descriptions of the various tests 
performed for this land boring exploration are presented below. 
Moisture and Density 
Moisture Content and Unit Weight tests were performed on selected soil samples to aid 
in defining the interface between the various soil layers encountered and correlating the layers 
between test borings. The moisture content and unit weight tests were performed in general 
accordance with ASTM D2216 and ASTM D7263, respectively. The test results are presented on 
the Logs of Borings at the appropriate sample depths. 
Atterberg Limits 
Thirteen Atterberg Limits tests were performed on selected soil samples to evaluate the 
liquid limit (LL), plastic limit (PL), and plasticity index (PI), as an aid in classifying the soils. The 
Atterberg Limits tests were performed in general accordance with ASTM D4318 (Method A). 
The test results are summarized on the Logs of Borings at the appropriate sample depths. 
Graphic presentations of the test results are provided on Plates G-1 and G-2. 
Sieve Analysis 
To provide information on the particle size distribution of the soils encountered, 
128 Sieve Analysis tests (ASTM D6912) were performed to evaluate the gradation 
characteristics and to aid in soil classification. Graphic presentations of the test results are 
provided on Plates G-3 through G-28. 
Specific Gravity 
Seventeen Specific gravity tests were performed on selected soil samples in general 
accordance with ASTM D854. The test results are presented on Plate G-29. 
GDR for HDR-H PMRF Site 4 - 02/28/2022
Appendix G 
Geotechnical Laboratory Tests 
W.O. 8192-00 
GEOLABS, INC. 
FEBRUARY 2022     Page G-2 
Hawaii • California 
Direct Shear 
To evaluate the shear strength characteristics of the in-situ soils, forty-four strain-
controlled, consolidated-drained Direct Shear tests were performed on the in-situ soil samples 
in accordance with ASTM D 3080. The test results are presented on Plates G-30 through G-81.  
Unconsolidated-Undrained Triaxial Compression 
To evaluate the undrained shear strength of the clayey soils, three Unconsolidated 
Undrained Triaxial Compression (TXUU) tests were performed on selected relatively 
undisturbed soil samples in accordance with ASTM D2850. The approximate in-situ effective 
overburden pressure was used as the applied confining pressure for the “undisturbed” samples. 
The test results and the stress-strain curves are presented on Plates G-82 through G-84. 
Consolidated-Undrained Triaxial Compression 
To evaluate the drained and undrained shear strengths of the subsurface soils, four 
Consolidated-Undrained Triaxial Compression tests were performed on selected, relatively 
undisturbed soil samples in accordance with ASTM D4767. The test results and stress-strain 
curves are presented on Plates D-85.1 through D-88.3. 
Uniaxial Compressive Strength – Rock Cores 
Forty Uniaxial Compressive Strength tests were performed on selected rock cores to 
evaluate their uniaxial compressive strength of the rock core in general accordance with 
ASTM D7012, Method C. Uniaxial compressive strength test results for the selected rock cores 
are presented on the Logs of Borings at the appropriate sample depths and also presented on 
Plates G-89 and G-90. 
Consolidation 
Forty-four Consolidation tests were performed on selected soil samples to evaluate the 
compressibility characteristics of the on-site soils. The tests were performed in accordance with 
ASTM D2435, and the test results are presented on Plates G-91 through G-135. 
Soil Resistivity and Chemical Analyses 
Twelve minimum resistivity (ASTM G57), pH (ASTM G51), chloride (EPA 300), and sulfate 
(EPA 300) content tests were performed by our office or Eurofins TestAmerica on selected soil 
samples obtained from our field exploration. The test results are presented on Plate G-136. 
Carbonate Content 
Twenty-nine Carbonate Content tests (ASTM D4373) were performed on selected 
samples of the in-situ soils to evaluate the carbonate content of the samples tested. The test 
results are summarized on Plates G-137 and D-138. 
GDR for HDR-H PMRF Site 4 - 02/28/2022
Appendix G 
Geotechnical Laboratory Tests 
W.O. 8192-00 
GEOLABS, INC. 
FEBRUARY 2022     Page G-3 
Hawaii • California 
Modified Proctor 
Six Modified Proctor compaction tests (ASTM D1557 A) were performed on bulk 
samples of the near-surface soils to evaluate the dry density and moisture content 
relationships. The test results are presented on Plates G-139 through G-144. 
California Bearing Ratio 
Six laboratory California Bearing Ratio tests (ASTM D1883) were performed on bulk 
samples of the near-surface soils to evaluate the pavement support characteristics of the soils. 
The test results are presented on Plates G-145 through D-150.

Hawaii • California 
A P P E N D I X   I 
Hammer Efficiency Calibration 
Standard penetration test energy measurement calibrations were performed for the 
drill rig and sampling hammers use on the project. The drill rig used for this project consisted of 
a CME-75DG2. The drill rig was equipped with an automatic-trip safety hammer. The energy 
delivered to the sampling rods were measured to determine the appropriate energy correction 
factor for the drill rig and hammer system. The dynamic testing was performed by Geolabs 
personnel in general accordance with ASTM D4633, “Standard Test Method for Energy 
Measurement for Dynamic Penetrometers.”  
Instrumentation 
For all the recorded tests, the instrumentation consisted of a 24-inch long AWJ rod 
subsection (1.75 OD/1.25 ID). The rod was equipped with two full-bridge foil resistance strain 
gages. Two piezo-resistive accelerometer transducers also were attached to the instrumented 
subsection. An SPT Analyzer Model SPT-8, manufactured by Pile Dynamics, Inc., was used to 
collect the data. The calibration and compliance certificates for the SPT Analyzer are presented 
in Figures I-1 and I-2 for information purposes only. The SPT Analyzer is a computer fitted with a 
data acquisition and signal conditioning system. During driving of the drill string, the strain and 
acceleration signals are recorded and processed for each hammer blow. The strain signal is 
converted to a force record, and the acceleration signal is converted to a velocity record. The 
SPT Analyzer saves selected hammer blows containing this information to disk and determines 
the compressive stresses, displacement, and energy at the point of measurement. This 
information can be viewed on the computer screen during driving. 
Energy Measurement 
The primary purpose of the testing was to determine the energy transferred from the 
hammer to the instrumented subsection. The SPT Analyzer measurements of force and velocity 
were analyzed to calculate the energy using the FV method (EFV), which is based on both force 
and velocity measurements as follows:  
EFV = ∫ F(t) v(t) dt 
Another method, known as the EF2 method, was not used due to the limitations 
associated with the measurements (i.e., based on force alone assuming proportionality at the 
gauge locations because velocity is not used in the analysis). The EF2 method is not the 
recommended method in ASTM D4633. 
GDR for HDR-H PMRF Site 4 - 02/28/2022
Appendix I 
Hammer Efficiency Calibration 
W.O. 8192-00 
GEOLABS, INC. 
FEBRUARY 2022     Page I-2 
Hawaii • California 
The rated energy (designated as ER) for the hammer is 0.35 kip-feet based on the 
2.5 feet (30 inch) drop height and 0.14 kip (140 pound) ram. The actual efficiency or energy 
transfer ratio (ETR) for the hammer system can then be determined using EFV and ER as 
follows:  
ETR = (EFV/ER)*100% 
For SPT, the typical ETR is taken as 60%, based on history and experience from prior 
studies. To adjust the measured SPT N values (Nmeasured) for actual hammer performance, the 
ETR can be used to correct the Nmeasured to N60 by using the correction factor (CN) determined as 
follows: 
CN = ETR/60% 
and N60 = CN * Nmeasured 
This is a correction for energy only. The application of additional corrections for 
overburden, non-conventional samplers, etc., was not considered in this study and should be 
evaluated for appropriateness by the geotechnical engineer for this project. 
Results 
The results of the testing program are presented in Plates I-1.1 through I-1.28 for drill rig 
CME-75DG2. The results include FMX (maximum measured force in the rod subsection), VMX 
(maximum measured velocity in the rod subsection), BPM (blows per minute), EFV, and ETR. 
The blow count, including the SPT N-value, and the soil description were recorded by Geolabs 
personnel. 
The data was presented with statistical information, including the average, maximum, 
minimum, and standard deviation for the blows associated with the group of data sets (sample 
depths) for the hammer. In addition, the data for all depths for a particular hammer combined 
are also presented in the tables. Note that the total blows may not match the number of blows 
analyzed due to differences between the blow count observations and the PDA recorded 
number. In some cases, bad blows or unreliable data from the PDA may have been omitted.  
The following table summarizes the hammer efficiency measurement results of the tests 
conducted for the drill rig (CME-75DG2) used on this project.

A P P E N D I X   J 
Modified California Sampler and Standard Penetration Test Sampler Blow Count Correlation 
Blow count correlation between the Standard Penetration Test (SPT) split-spoon 
sampler and the Modified California split barrel sampler was developed with side-by-side 
borings using both samplers at the same depths. The following table summarizes the samplers 
used in the side-by-side correlation. 
SAMPLERS 
Sampler Type 
Outer Diameter 
(inches) 
Inner Diameter 
(inches) 
Standard Penetration Test 
(SPT) Sampler  
2.0 
1.375 
Modified California Sampler 
(MCS) 
3.0 
2.42 
(fitted with internal rings or 
brass liners) 
The SPT results typically are reported as the N-value, which is defined as the number of 
blows required to drive a standard split-spoon sampler 12 inches with a 140-pound hammer 
after 6 inches of initial seating. The penetration resistance of the larger diameter modified 
California sampler is also recorded as blows per 12 inches and can be approximately related to 
the SPT N-value through correlation to determine an equivalent SPT N-value. 
The SPT N-values used for this comparison were corrected based on the efficiency of the 
hammer-rod energy transfer for the various drill rigs used to drive each sampler and are 
presented as N60 values on the SPT-MCS Blow Count Correlation Table. A total of forty (40) 
driven samples from our other projects were used to develop the side-by-side correlation. The 
driven samples were obtained in various deposits consisting of coral detritus, recent and older 
alluvium, estuarine and lagoonal deposits, and beach deposits. The data used for the side-by-
side correlation is presented on the Modified California Sampler Versus Standard Penetration 
Test Sampler Correlation Table, Table J-1. 
Correlation between the SPT and MCS drive samples was developed by comparing 
N60 values obtained from each sampler at the same depth in side-by-side borings. The 
side-by-side borings were located a maximum distance of about 15 feet apart. In addition, 
N60 values from the SPT and MCS samples were compared within the same boring at depths no 
greater than 5 feet and in the same material. Each SPT-MCS comparison was carefully selected 
to ensure uniformity of soil consistency and stratigraphy between each sampling event. 
Based on the results of the side-by-side comparison, a regression analysis of the 
presented data suggest that the equivalent SPT N-values would be about 56 percent of those 
recorded with the Modified California Sampler. A data plot with the regression analysis plotting 
GDR for HDR-H PMRF Site 4 - 02/28/2022
